معلول له. ثانيتهما أن العلة هذه إذا وجدت في أي فرع آخر، أنتجت نفس الحكم، أي أن العلة الواحدة تحدث دائماً معلولاً واحداً. وهاتان القضيتان أو المقدمتان من الصعوبة بمكان القطع بهما لأن العلتين علة الأصل وعلة الفرع متشابهتان ولا بد من تغاير الشبيهين وإلا أصبح كل منهما عين الآخر فيكونان أمراً واحداً والمفروض أنهما شبيهان. وإذا اختلفت هويتهما، يحتمل أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف مانعاً من الجمع بينهما. يجيب بعض الأصوليين: بأن اختلاف الهوية المشخصة ليس مطلقاً عقبة في سبيل الجمع بين العلتين: لأن القائس لا ينظر إلى خصوصيات المحال، ولكن إلى عمومياتها فيقطع بهذا لا عقلاً فحسب ولكن عقلاً وعادة وشرعاً. أما من الناحية العقلية: فلأنا نقطع أن المحل إنما يصير أسود أو أبيض أو عالماً لعموم هذه المعاني ولا يقطع مطلقاً لشخصياتها. أما من ناحية العادة: فلأنا نعلم أن زيداً احترق بيته بهذه النار لأنه هذا البيت، ولا لأنها هذه النار. ولكن لأن هذا بيت وهذه نار. أما خصوص البيت وخصوص النار فلا صلة له مطلقاً بالإحراق. أما من الناحية الشرعية: فاننا نرجم الزاني لا لخصوص زناه، ولكن لما صدر منه مفهوم الزنا المطلق فاذا ما نظرنا إلى هذه العموميات ولم ننظر إلى الجزئي أو المشخص، أمكن الجمع بالعلة ـ وهنا يصل الباحث إلى درجة اليقين. د ـ الاعتراض الرابع ـ وهو أنه على فرض إفادته لليقين، فان القياس المنطقي الارسططاليسي يغني عنه، وهذا نقد من وجهة نظر أرسططاليسية بحتة ـ ومع ذلك فقد رد بعض الأصوليين بأنه يمكن اعتبار قياس الغائب على
Some earlier and later theologians criticize this method and show its weakness and its failure to lead to certainty—certainty being the goal of rational sciences. The reasons for its weakness are summarized as follows: A) The combination by cause is false, because according to the Ash'aris there is no cause and no effect. The intention of denying combination by cause is to deny it from a rational perspective, because we have seen that the Ash'aris themselves acknowledged the principle of causality in usul al-fiqh reasoning. Ibn Rushd does not accept this criticism directed at reasoning from the unseen to the witnessed on the grounds that there is no connection or necessity between condition and conditioned, or between a thing and its cause and evidence. Then he traces all this back to the Sophists. He asserts that the theologians, headed by Imam al-Haramayn, were influenced in this criticism by these Sophists. Ibn Rushd is not so much bothered by the criticism of reasoning from the unseen to the witnessed itself as he is by the criticism of the necessary relationship between cause and effect. B) The second reason for the weakness of this method is that combination by essence is false, because temporal knowledge contradicts eternal knowledge; how can they combine in essence and definition despite their difference? Many theologians do not accept this criticism, because temporal and eternal knowledge can be combined under the same concept of knowledge, but Imam al-Haramayn insists that affirming a common concept of knowledge is based on affirming states (ahwal), and the doctrine of states is false. C) The third reason for the weakness of this method is that it does not yield certainty, because the strongest type of this analogy is combination by cause, and combination by cause does not bring the reasoner to certainty, because combination by cause relies on two premises that are assumed: first, that the ruling is established in the original case due to a specific cause, or that this specific cause produced this effect—so the ruling is its effect. Second, that this cause, if found in any other branch, produces the same ruling, i.e., one cause always produces one effect. These two propositions are difficult to be certain about because the two causes—the cause of the original and the cause of the branch—are similar, and similar things must be different, otherwise each would be the same as the other and they would be one thing, whereas they are assumed to be similar. If their identities differ, it is possible that this difference prevents the combination between them. Some usul scholars answer: The difference in individual identity is not an absolute obstacle to combining the two causes, because the reasoner does not look at particularities of instances but at their universalities, and so becomes certain about this not only rationally but also customarily and legally. Rationally: we are certain that a substrate becomes black or white or knowledgeable due to the universality of these meanings, and not absolutely due to their particularities. Customarily: we know that Zayd's house burned down with this fire because it is this house, not because it is this fire, but because this is a house and this is fire. The particularity of the house and the particularity of the fire have absolutely no connection to burning. Legally: we stone the adulterer not because of the particularity of his adultery, but because of the absolute concept of adultery that emanated from him. So if we look at these universalities and do not look at the particular or the individual, combination by cause becomes possible—and here the researcher reaches certainty. D) The fourth objection—that assuming it yields certainty, Aristotelian logical syllogism suffices for it, and this is a criticism from a purely Aristotelian perspective—nevertheless, some usul scholars have replied that reasoning from the unseen to the witnessed can be considered
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage critically examines the method of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen), which theologians use to infer divine attributes. The author lists four reasons why critics deem it weak, then provides responses from some usul scholars. The first reason (a) is that 'combination by cause' (al-jam' bi al-'illa) is false because Ash'aris deny causality; however, Ibn Rushd retorts that this denial leads to sophistry. The second reason (b) is that 'combination by essence' (al-jam' bi al-haqiqa) is false because temporal and eternal knowledge differ essentially; Imam al-Haramayn rejects the reply that they share a common concept of knowledge since that relies on the doctrine of states (ahwal), which he deems invalid. The third reason (c) is that the method does not yield certainty because the two required premises—that a specific cause produces a specific effect in the original case, and that the same cause always produces the same effect—are difficult to ascertain, as similar causes may differ in ways that prevent equivalence. In response, some usul scholars argue that only universal aspects matter (not particularities), and certainty can be attained rationally, customarily, and legally. The fourth objection (d) is that Aristotelian syllogism makes this method redundant; however, the response is cut off.

Key Terms
الجمع بالحقيقة
العلم الحادث
العلم القديم
الأحوال
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Haramayn reject the response that both temporal and eternal knowledge share the same concept of knowledge?
A) Because he denies the existence of knowledge.
B) Because that concept depends on the doctrine of states (ahwal), which he rejects.
C) Because he believes temporal knowledge is superior.
D) Because the response is from a non-Ash'ari.

Key Takeaway
The second critique shows that theological commitments (like rejecting states) can block attempts to use common essences in analogical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'Human knowledge is a property of a contingent being; God's knowledge is a property of the Necessary Being. Therefore, they cannot share the same essence.' Reconstruct al-Juwayni's argument in support of this claim.

The Third Critique: Certainty and the Problem of Similar Causes
Author presents third critique and response from usul scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the third critique that combination by cause does not yield certainty and evaluate the response that focusing on universals restores certainty.

Core Idea
The third critique challenges the certainty of analogical reasoning because similar causes may differ, but the response argues that universal aspects, not particularities, ensure certainty.

The third reason for weakness is that even the strongest form of analogy (combination by cause) does not yield certainty because it requires two difficult premises: that a specific cause produces a specific effect, and that the same cause always produces the same effect. Since the cause in the original and branch are similar but not identical, their difference may prevent the inference. Some usul scholars respond that the reasoner only considers universal aspects, not particularities, and this yields certainty rationally, customarily, and legally.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two premises required for certainty
The third critique says that combination by cause relies on two premises: (1) the ruling in the original case is due to a specific cause, and (2) that same cause produces the same ruling in any branch. These are hard to establish.
The passage calls these 'مقدمتين مسلمتين'.
2
Identify the problem of similar causes
The two causes (original and branch) are similar but must be different; if they were identical, they would be one thing. But their difference may prevent the analogy.
This is a logical problem: the similarity required for analogy coexists with necessary distinction.
3
Examine the response: focusing on universals
Some usul scholars argue that the reasoner does not look at particularities (الأشخاص) but at universalities (العموميات). For example, we are certain that a house burns because it is a house and fire because it is fire, not because of particularities.
The response uses examples from reason ('aql), custom ('ada), and law (shar').
4
Evaluate whether this response achieves certainty
The passage concludes that when looking at universals, combination by cause becomes possible and the researcher reaches certainty (اليقين). Consider if this is convincing.

Induction and Analogy

Universality vs. Particularity in Analogical Reasoning
Author responds to objections against analogical reasoning and explains its epistemic basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how focusing on universal aspects over particular identities allows analogical reasoning to achieve certainty.

Core Idea
Certainty in analogy comes from universals, not particulars.

The passage addresses a key objection to analogical reasoning: that the difference in specific identity between the original and branch cases prevents certainty. In response, some usulis argue that the analogizer considers universal aspects, not particularities. This lesson unpacks that response and its implications for theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate in the passage the specific objection against analogical reasoning: that the two causes are similar yet distinct, and their different identities might prevent joining them.
The objection hinges on al-huwiyya al-mushakhkhasa (specific identity).
2
Understand the usuli response
Read the usuli reply: the analogizer looks at universalities (عموميات) not particularities. Certainty is possible rationally, customarily, and legally.
The response is threefold.
3
Analyze the three levels of proof
Break down each level: rationally – a subject's properties are due to universal meanings; customarily – fire burns a house because of universal categories; legally – the adulterer is stoned for the universal concept of adultery.
Each level shows that particularities are irrelevant.
4
Synthesize the argument
Conclude that by focusing on universals, the analogizer reaches certainty, because the cause ('illa) is universal and not tied to individual identity.
This synthesis is the core of the defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى درجة اليقين ، لأن الجمع بالعلة يستند إلى مقدمتين مسلمتين أولاهما ـ أن الحكم ثبت في الأصل لعلة معينة أو أن هذه العلة المعينة أنتجت هذا المعلول ـ فالحكم معلول له . ثانيتهما ـ أن العلة هذه إذا وجدت في أي فرع آخر ، أنتجت نفس الحكم ، أي أن العلة الواحدة تحدث دائماً معلولاً واحداً . وهاتان القضيتان أو المقدمتان من الصعوبة بمكان القطع بهما لأن العلتين ـ علة الأصل وعلة الفرع ـ متشابهتان ولا بد من تغاير الشبيهين وإلا أصبح كل منهما عين الآخر فيكونان أمراً واحداً والمفروض أنهما شبيهان . وإذا اختلفت هويتهما ، يحتمل أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف مانعاً من الجمع بينهما(١) . يجيب بعض الأصوليين : بأن اختلاف الهوية المشخصة ليس مطلقاً عقبة في سبيل الجمع بين العلتين : لأن القائس لا ينظر إلى خصوصيات المحال ، ولكن إلى عمومياتها فيقطع بهذا لا عقلاً فحسب ولكن عقلاً وعادة وشرعاً . أما من الناحية العقلية : فلأنا نقطع أن المحل إنما يصير أسود أو أبيض أو عالماً لعموم هذه المعاني ولا يقطع مطلقاً لشخصياتها . أما من ناحية العادة : فلأنا نعلم أن زيداً احترق بيته بهذه النار لأنه هذا البيت ، ولا لأنها هذه النار . ولكن لأن هذا بيت وهذه نار . أما خصوص البيت وخصوص النار فلا صلة له مطلقاً بالإحراق . أما من الناحية الشرعية : فاننا نرجم الزاني لا لخصوص زناه ، ولكن لما صدر منه مفهوم الزنا المطلق فاذا ما نظرنا إلى هذه العموميات ولم ننظر إلى الجزئي أو المشخص ، أمكن الجمع بالعلة ـ وهنا يصل الباحث إلى درجة اليقين(٢) . د ـ الاعتراض الرابع ـ وهو أنه على فرض إفادته لليقين ، فان القياس المنطقي الارسططاليسي يغني عنه ، وهذا نقد من وجهة نظر أرسططاليسية بحتة ـ ومع ذلك فقد رد بعض الأصوليين بأنه يمكن اعتبار قياس الغائب على الشاهد دليلاً بجانب الدليل البرهاني ، فيمكن به الترجيح عند التعارض(١) . هذه صورة لقياس الغائب على الشاهد لدى علماء الكلام ، وهو في جوهره القياس الأصولي ، ولكن يمتاز عنه بتلك الجوامع التي ذكرنا .
To the degree of certainty, because joining by cause relies on two conceded premises: First, that the ruling is established in the source for a specific cause, or that this specific cause produced this effect—so the ruling is an effect of it. Second, that if this cause is found in any other branch, it produces the same ruling, i.e., a single cause always produces a single effect. These two propositions or premises are difficult to be certain about because the two causes—the cause of the source and the cause of the branch—are similar, and it is necessary that the two similar things be distinct; otherwise each would be the identical other, making them one thing, but they are assumed to be similar. If their identities differ, that difference might prevent joining them. Some usulis answer: The difference in specific identity is not an absolute obstacle to joining the two causes, because the one drawing the analogy does not look at the particularities of the subjects but at their universalities, and thus he is certain about this not only rationally but also rationally, customarily, and legally. Rationally: We are certain that a subject becomes black or white or knowledgeable due to the universality of these meanings, not absolutely for their particularities. Customarily: We know that Zayd's house burned by this fire because it is this house, not because it is this fire; but because it is a house and this is fire. The particularity of the house and the particularity of the fire have absolutely no connection to burning. Legally: We stone the adulterer not for the particularity of his adultery, but because of the absolute concept of adultery that issued from him. So if we look to these universalities and not to the particular or specific, joining by cause becomes possible—and here the researcher reaches certainty. Fourth objection: That assuming it yields certainty, the Aristotelian logical syllogism suffices for it, and this is a critique from a purely Aristotelian perspective. Nevertheless, some usulis responded that the analogy of the unseen to the seen can be considered a proof alongside the demonstrative proof, thus it can be used for preference when there is conflict. This is an image of the analogy of the unseen to the seen among the theologians, and it is essentially the usuli analogy, but it is distinguished by those comprehensive aspects we mentioned.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid) as used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. The author explains that joining two cases by their cause (al-jam' bi al-'illa) depends on two premises: first, that the ruling in the original case is caused by a specific 'illa; second, that the same 'illa produces the same ruling in any branch. The difficulty in certainty arises because the cause in the original and branch are similar yet distinct; if they were identical, the two cases would be one. Critics argue that the difference in identity precludes certainty. In response, some usulis argue that the analogizer considers universal aspects, not particularities, and certainty is attainable from rational, customary, and legal perspectives. Rationally, a subject's property (like blackness) is due to universal meaning, not particular identity. Customarily, fire burns a house because it is a house and fire, regardless of their particularities. Legally, an adulterer is stoned for the universal concept of adultery. By focusing on universals, the analogizer achieves certainty. A fourth objection from Aristotelian logic claims the syllogism suffices, but usulis reply that this analogy can stand alongside demonstrative proof and serve as a preferred argument when evidence conflicts. The passage concludes by describing qiyas al-gha'ib as essentially the usuli analogy distinguished by the comprehensive aspects discussed.

Key Terms
الجمع بالعلة
العلة
الهوية المشخصة
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

The usuli defense of analogy shows that certainty is possible by focusing on the universal cause rather than the particular identities of the cases.

Analogical Proof vs. Syllogistic Proof: A Defense of Qiyas al-Gha'ib
Author responds to objections against analogical reasoning and explains its epistemic basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the role of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-gha'ib) from Aristotelian syllogism and explain why it remains a valid proof.

Core Idea
Analogy is a supplementary proof, not replaced by syllogism.

The fourth objection in the passage claims that the Aristotelian syllogism suffices, rendering analogical reasoning unnecessary. The usuli response asserts that analogical reasoning can serve as a proof alongside demonstrative proof, especially for preference when conflicts arise. This lesson examines that response and the unique role of qiyas al-gha'ib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth objection
Find in the passage the objection from an Aristotelian perspective: that if analogical reasoning yields certainty, the Aristotelian syllogism already suffices for that purpose.
This objection challenges the need for a separate analogical method.
2
Grasp the usuli response
Read the usuli reply: analogical reasoning can be considered a proof alongside the demonstrative proof, and it can be used for preference (tarjih) when proofs conflict.
The response does not reject syllogism but gives analogy a complementary role.
3
Compare the two methods
Contrast the structure of Aristotelian syllogism (deductive, categorical) with qiyas al-gha'ib (analogical, based on 'illa). Note that analogy extends knowledge from the known to the unknown.
Analogy is not reducible to syllogism because it requires identification of a common cause.
4
Evaluate the epistemological status
Assess why analogy can still produce certainty (as argued earlier) and why it is considered a valid tool alongside syllogism in Islamic sciences.
The passage emphasizes that analogy is essentially the usuli analogy (qiyas usuli) distinguished by comprehensive aspects.

To the degree of certainty, because joining by cause relies on two conceded premises: First, that the ruling is established in the source for a specific cause, or that this specific cause produced this effect—so the ruling is an effect of it. Understanding Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid
Author defines qiyas al-gha'ib and its role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure and function of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid and distinguish it from demonstrative proof and legal analogy.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-gha'ib is a tool for weighing conflicting proofs, not a demonstrative certainty.

In this passage, the author introduces the first logical method used by theologians: inference from the present (shahid) to the absent (gha'ib). This method is a type of evidence that operates alongside demonstrative proof, and its main function is to help weigh preferences when proofs conflict. Understanding this distinction is crucial for following theological arguments and debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two realms
Recognize that 'al-shahid' refers to the observable, present world, and 'al-gha'ib' refers to the unobservable, absent realm (e.g., Allah, the afterlife). The analogy moves from a known attribute in shahid to a corresponding attribute in gha'ib.
In kalam, these terms are standard; shahid is the basis for inference.
2
Understand its function
The passage states that qiyas al-gha'ib serves as a proof 'alongside demonstrative proof' and is used for tarjih (weighing) when proofs conflict. It does not replace demonstrative proof but assists in preference.
Tarjih is a technical term in usul al-fiqh and kalam for preferring one evidence over another.
3
Relate to legal analogy (qiyas usuli)
The author notes that qiyas al-gha'ib is essentially the same as the qiyas of usul al-fiqh, but distinguished by 'comprehensive principles' (jawaami'). These principles likely include broader metaphysical or theological premises that govern the analogy in kalam.
In usul al-fiqh, qiyas transfers a ruling based on a common 'illa; here the 'illa may be a theological attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشاهد دليلاً بجانب الدليل البرهاني ، فيمكن به الترجيح عند التعارض(١) . هذه صورة لقياس الغائب على الشاهد لدى علماء الكلام ، وهو في جوهره القياس الأصولي ، ولكن يمتاز عنه بتلك الجوامع التي ذكرنا .
The witnessed [evidence] is a proof alongside the demonstrative proof, so it can be used to weigh (preferences) when there is conflict(1). This is the form of analogical reasoning from the absent to the present among the theologians, and it is in essence the analogical reasoning of usul al-fiqh, but it is distinguished from it by those comprehensive principles that we mentioned.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage introduces the first logical method: qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (inference from the present to the absent). The author states that this form of evidence serves as a proof alongside demonstrative proof (burhan), and it can be used to weigh preferences when two proofs conflict. He then clarifies that this is the form of analogy used by the theologians (mutakallimun). In its essence, it is the same as the analogical reasoning (qiyas) of Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh), but it differs by the 'comprehensive principles' (jawaami') mentioned earlier (presumably in the text). These principles likely refer to broader metaphysical or theological premises that govern the analogy. The passage defines the method and its relationship to legal analogy.

The Method of Inference from Agreed Upon to Disputed
Author defines and critiques the method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of inference from the agreed upon to the disputed as a form of analogical reasoning and evaluate its applicability in legal versus rational sciences.

Core Idea
Inference from consensus is valid in law but invalid in rational sciences.

In Islamic legal theory, consensus (ijma') is a binding source. Some thinkers extended this method to rational matters, arguing that what is agreed upon can settle disputes. This lesson unpacks that method, its example, and its reduction to the more fundamental analogy from the present to the absent. We will see why later theorists rejected it for rational sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define the method
From the Arabic, identify what 'inference from the agreed upon to the disputed' means. Look at the term 'mustadill' (one who infers) and 'mukhtalaf fihi' (disputed matter). The method uses a point of agreement as a premise to argue for a disputed conclusion.
The passage explicitly labels this the third method.
2
Examine the example given
The author gives an example: drawing an analogy between colors (accidents) and modes (akwan, like movement/rest) to show that substances cannot be free from them. This is an analogical argument: if colors are necessary accidents for substances, then modes should be too, because both are accidents. The agreed upon (colors) is used to infer the disputed (modes).
The example comes from kalam discussions on accidents.
3
Reduce to qiyas al-gha'ib
The author states that ultimately this method is just analogy from the present to the absent (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). The agreed upon is the witnessed (shahid) and the disputed is the absent (gha'ib). So it is not a distinct method but a subtype of the first method.
This reduction is key to the critique.
4
Compare legal and rational domains
Imam al-Haramayn's definition for legal reasoning is 'subsuming the disputed under the agreed upon.' In law, consensus is authoritative. But in rational matters (al-ma'qulat), the goal is certain knowledge (al-'ilm), and agreement or disagreement among scholars has no bearing on truth. Therefore the method fails for rational sciences.
The passage explicitly rejects its use in al-ma'qulat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث : الإستدلال بالمتفق عليه على المختلف فيه(١) ويمثلون له بقياس الألوان على الأكوان في استحالة تعري الجواهر عنها . فهذا الطريق - في آخر تحليل له - ليس إلا قياس الغائب على الشاهد . ويعرفه إمام الحرمين في تقاسيمه للنظر الشرعي بالالحاق المختلف فيه بالمجتمع المتفق عليه(٢) . أما الاستدلال بالمتفق عليه على المختلف فيه في المعقولات ، فلا يقبله أيضاً النظار المتأخرون . فان المطلوب في المعقولات العلم ، ولا أثر للخلاف والوفاق في المعقولات(٣) .
The third method: Inference from what is agreed upon to what is disputed(1). They give as an example the analogy of colors upon modes (akwan) regarding the impossibility of substances being free from them. So this method, in its final analysis, is nothing but analogy from the present to the absent. Imam al-Haramayn defines it in his divisions of legal reasoning as subsuming the disputed under the agreed upon(2). As for inference from the agreed upon to the disputed in rational matters, the later theorists do not accept it either. For what is sought in rational matters is knowledge, and disagreement and agreement have no effect in rational matters(3).
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage describes the third method: inference from what is agreed upon to what is disputed. An example is given: analogizing colors (which are accidents) to modes (akwan, i.e., types of accidents like movement/rest) to prove that substances cannot be free from them. The author then analyzes this method as essentially a form of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (the first method). Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) defines it in legal reasoning as 'subsuming the disputed under the agreed upon.' However, the later theorists (mutakallimun) reject this method in rational matters because the goal in rational inquiry is certain knowledge (al-'ilm), and agreement or disagreement among scholars has no bearing on the truth in rational sciences. Thus, while it might be used in legal reasoning (where consensus matters), it is invalid for rational/metaphysical arguments. The passage critiques the method by highlighting its reliance on consensus rather than proof.

Key Terms
المتفق عليه
المختلف فيه
المعقولات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is only applicable to legal reasoning.
B) Because agreement and disagreement do not affect the truth in rational matters.
C) Because the example of colors and modes is invalid.
D) Because it reduces to qiyas al-gha'ib which is also rejected.

Key Takeaway
In rational sciences, truth is independent of consensus; inference based on agreement is epistemologically invalid.

Al-Ilzamat: Method or Form of Qiyas?
Author critiques and compares al-Razi's and al-Iji's views on al-Ilzamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the nature of al-Ilzamat as a method for establishing cause and analyze the disagreement between al-Razi and al-Iji regarding its classification.

Core Idea
Al-Ilzamat: turning the opponent's own admission into a logical proof, but is it an independent method or a form of qiyas?

In this lesson, we examine the fifth way of establishing cause, al-Ilzamat (implications). The passage presents differing views from al-Razi, al-Iji, and the commentator of al-Mawaqif, highlighting a key methodological debate in Islamic logic. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate analogical arguments in juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of qiyas
Recognize qiyas al-tard (extending ruling by similarity) and qiyas al-'aks (inference by contrapositive). Note that al-Razi considers al-Ilzamat as a type of qiyas taking these forms.
Al-Razi's Nihayat al-'Uqul is the source.
2
Compare al-Razi and al-Iji
Al-Razi treats al-Ilzamat as analogical reasoning (qiyas) that can be positive or negative. Al-Iji treats it as a distinct method for establishing the common cause in analogy of unseen from seen. How do their definitions differ?
3
Analyze the commentator's critique
The commentator of al-Mawaqif argues that al-Iji erred in considering al-Ilzamat a third method; in al-Razi's division, it is a third independent method, not a part of the other two. Understand the nuance.
The weakness lies in attributing cause identification to the opponent.
4
Consider the reconciling opinion
Another view suggests al-Ilzamat can be both an independent method and a type of proof of common cause, based on the assumption that the opponent admits the cause.
This avoids the weakness of attribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الخامس الإلزامات :
وقد اختلف أيضاً في هذا الطريق . فالرازي يقرر في نهاية العقول أن
الإلزامات « من أنواع القياس بالحقيقة وأنه يكون أحياناً على صورة « قياس

العكس . أو بمعنى آخر هو الاستدلال القياسي بانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء
الملزوم . أما صاحب المواقف فيعالجه على أنه « من أشهر الطرق في إثبات

The fifth way: Al-Ilzamat (Implications). There has been disagreement about this way. Al-Razi decisively states in Nihayat al-'Uqul that al-Ilzamat are from the types of qiyas in reality, and that it sometimes takes the form of 'qiyas al-tard' i.e., extending the ruling of the original case to the branch case, whether the ruling is positive (so the extension is in affirmation) or negative (so the extension is in negation). Or in other words, it is analogical reasoning by verifying the implication of the existence of the antecedent to verify the existence of the consequent, and sometimes it takes the form of qiyas al-'aks. Or in other words, it is analogical reasoning by negating the consequent to negate the antecedent. As for al-Iji (sahib al-Mawaqif), he treats it as 'one of the most famous methods in establishing the common cause' in the analogy of the unseen based on the seen. He defines it as 'it is analogy upon what the opponent asserts, with a distinguishing cause that exists in the original which the opponent asserts and does not exist in the branch being analogized to.' Because if the intention of al-Ilzamat is qiyas al-tard or qiyas al-'aks, these analogies are not weak, but weakness comes from our attributing the identification of the cause and the ruling to the opponent. Then the opponent comes and denies one of them, i.e., denies that the identification of the ruling was for this cause, and that the identification of the cause was for this ruling. As for the commentator of al-Mawaqif, he sees that al-Iji erred in considering 'al-Ilzamat as a third of the methods proving the cause' while they are—in al-Razi's division from whom al-Iji derived his statement regarding what is preferable—intended as a third independent method. There is another opinion that inclines to say that there is absolutely no objection to considering al-Ilzamat, like al-sabr wa al-taqsim, as an independent method on one hand, and at the same time it includes a type of proof of the common cause, because the person making the analogy adheres to it assuming that his opponent acknowledges the ruling of the original and its causality that this analogizer claims is the cause, and therefore he does not resort to another method in proving the causality of the cause of the ruling of the original. Thus the assumption of the opponent's admission of the causality of the cause of the ruling of the original is a third method in proving the causality of the cause of the analogy.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth way, al-Ilzamat, and presents differing views from al-Razi, al-Iji, and the commentator of al-Mawaqif. It begins by stating that there is disagreement about this way. Al-Razi, in his work Nihayat al-'Uqul, argues that al-Ilzamat are truly a type of qiyas (analogical reasoning). He says it can take the form of qiyas al-tard (analogy of running the same ruling) or qiyas al-'aks (analogy of inversion). In other words, it is reasoning from the existence of the antecedent to the existence of the consequent (positive) or from the negation of the consequent to the negation of the antecedent (negative). So according to al-Razi, it is a form of deductive analogy. In contrast, al-Iji (sahib al-Mawaqif) treats it as a well-known method for establishing the common cause in the analogy of the unseen based on the seen. He defines it as an analogy that uses the opponent's own admission, but with a distinguishing cause that exists in the original case (which the opponent accepts) and not in the branch case. This means the opponent's own principle is turned against him. The passage then notes that if al-Ilzamat were simply qiyas al-tard or qiyas al-'aks, those are not weak, but the weakness lies in attributing the identification of cause and ruling to the opponent. The opponent can then deny one of them. The commentator of al-Mawaqif criticizes al-Iji for mistake: in al-Razi's division, al-Ilzamat is intended as a third independent method, not as part of the two. Another opinion reconciles by saying al-Ilzamat can be considered both an independent method and a type of proof of the common cause, because the analogizer assumes the opponent's admission of the cause, avoiding other methods.

Induction and Analogy

Al-Ghazali's Defense of Using Fiqh Examples in Logic
Al-Ghazali defends using fiqh examples in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Ghazali's argument for using probabilistic fiqh examples to teach certain rational matters and evaluate its epistemological grounds.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali defends using fiqh examples by arguing that logic must be taught through the familiar, even if it yields probability (zann) not certainty.

Al-Ghazali faced criticism for using probabilistic (zanni) fiqh examples to illustrate certain logical matters. In this passage, he anticipates this objection and offers a robust defense based on pedagogical necessity and the nature of human learning. Understanding his argument reveals how he sought to integrate Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences without demanding demonstrative certainty in every domain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection al-Ghazali anticipates
Find the criticism he mentions: that using zanni fiqh examples to explain certain rational matters is a deviation from custom. He calls the critic 'the beholder with the blind eye'.
2
Extract his defense: the carpenter analogy
Al-Ghazali argues that just as a carpenter learns best through examples from carpentry, a jurist learns logic best through fiqh examples. The goal is to move from the known to the unknown.
Note the emphasis on the addressee's background.
3
Identify his epistemological principle
He states: 'How can a person attain the unknown except through what is known to him?' This is the core pedagogical principle: learning proceeds from the familiar to the unfamiliar.
This principle applies across disciplines: fiqh, kalam, philosophy.
4
Assess the epistemological shift
Al-Ghazali accepts that fiqh examples yield only probability (zann), not certainty (yaqin). He argues that for logic to be useful in disciplines like fiqh, one must accept that its applications may not always produce certain knowledge.
This is a significant departure from the Aristotelian demand for certainty in demonstration.

ينكر انحرافنا عن العادات في تفهيم العقليات القطعية بالأمثلة الفقهية الظنية ، فليكف عن غلوائه . في طعنه وإزرائه . وليشهد على نفسه بالجهل بصناعة التمثيل ، وفائدتها لم توضع إلا لتفهم الأمر الخفي بما هو الأعرف عند المخاطب المسترشد ، ليقيس مجهوله إلى ما هو معلوم عنده فيستقر المجهول في نفسه ، كان الخطاب مع نجار لا يحسن إلا النجر وكيفية استعمال آلاته ، وجب على مرشده ألا يضرب له المثل إلا من صناعة النجارة ، ليكون ذلك أسبق الى فهمه وأقرب إلى مناسبة عقله »(۲) . ويرى الغزالي أن هذه هي الطريقة الوحيدة لإدخال المنطق في تلك العلوم واتخاذه قانوناً لها . وكيف يتوصل الإنسان إلى المجهول إلا بما هو معلوم عنده ، فالفقیه بما لديه من فقه ، والمتكلم بما لديه من كلام ، والفيلسوف بما لديه من فلسفة . ولا يضع الغزالي أمثلة فقهية فقط في هذا الكتاب ، بل يحلل الإستقراء الناقص والقياس التمثيلي على أنهما طريقان من طرق الفقه التي لا توصل إلى اليقين بل إلى الظن ، وهذا هو المطلوب في الفقه . وقصور الأول عن اليقين أنه لم يتصفح الجزئيات تصفحاً لا يشذ عنه شيء ، ولكن إذا كثرت الأصول قوي الظن ، ومهما ازدادت الأصول الشاهدة - أعني الجزئيات - اختلافاً ، كان الظن أقوى فيه »(۳) فمثلاً يقول الشافعية : إن مسح الرأس وظيفة أصلية في الوضوء فيستحب تكرارها واستندوا في هذا إلى استقراء من غسل الوجه واليدين والرجلين . فاذا عارض الاحناف بأنه « مسح لا يكرر » لاستقرائهم ذلك من التيمم ومسح الخف ، كان ظن الاحناف أقوى لدلالة جزئين مختلفين عليه ، بينما الأعضاء الثلاثة في الوضوء في حكم شاهد واحد لتجانسهما . ففي الإستقراء الفقهي إذن عنصر آخر لم يعرفه الإستقراء الارسططاليسي ، هو ترجيح سلسلة من الجزئيات على سلسلة أخرى للوصول إلى الحكم الكلي « إن قصور الإستقراء عن الكمال أوجب قصور الاعتقاد الحاصل على اليقين ، ولم يوجب بقاء الاحتمال على التعادل ، كما كان ، بل رجح بالظن أحد الاحتمالين »(۱) . فالإستقراء الفقهي إذن عنصران : إستقراء سلاسل الجزئيات أولاً ، ثم ترجيح بعض تلك السلاسل على الأخرى ، واستخلاص الحكم العام نتيجة لهذا الترجيح ثانياً . أما ضعف القياس التمثيلي فناتج عن أن المقدمة الكبرى فيه ظنية . ثم يعالج الغزالي هذا القياس علاجاً أصولياً بحتاً(٢) . ثم يبحث بعد ذلك في قياس العلة وهو قياس اللم عند المنطقيين ، وقياس الإن عند المنطقيين ، ويدعمهما بأمثلة فقهية(۳) ، ففي المعيار إذن نقد لمادة المقدمات نقد للمعرفة الإنسانية . وهو في بحثه للعلية في هذا الكتاب لا ينكرها بمعناها المطلق . وليس في المعيار ـ ما في التهافت من نظرية نقدية رائعة ، بل هو تعبير عن روح يونانية . أما في محك النظر ، فلا يغلب على الكتاب الجانب الكلامي ، بل تسوده أبحاث أصولية . فيشرح قياس الدلالة والعلة(٤) . ثم يتكلم بعد ذلك عن مدارك الأقيسة الفقهية(٥) . ويلاحظ على هذا الكتاب أن الغزالي يبدأ في
The standard of knowledge: first direction. Al-Ghazali says: 'We desired to present in the curriculum of speech in this chapter fiqh examples so that its benefit may encompass and its utility and benefit may include all categories' (1). And he indicates that there are those who will criticize him for making certain rational matters understood through probabilistic fiqh examples. He says: 'Perhaps the beholder with the blind eye, the view of criticism and disparagement, will deny our deviation from customs in making certain rational matters understood through probabilistic fiqh examples. Let him cease his excess in his criticism and disparagement, and let him testify against himself as ignorant of the art of analogy. Its benefit is only placed to make a hidden matter understood through what is more known to the guided addressee, so that he may measure his unknown to what is known to him, and the unknown becomes settled in his soul. If the speech were to a carpenter who is only skilled in carpentry and the use of his tools, it would be incumbent on his guide not to strike an example for him except from the craft of carpentry, so that it may be quicker to his understanding and closer to the suitability of his mind' (2). Al-Ghazali sees that this is the only way to introduce logic into those sciences and adopt it as a law for them. How can a person attain the unknown except through what is known to him? So the jurist with his jurisprudence, the theologian with his theology, and the philosopher with his philosophy. Al-Ghazali does not place only fiqh examples in this book, but he analyzes incomplete induction and analogical reasoning as two methods of fiqh that do not lead to certainty but to probability, and this is what is required in fiqh. 'The deficiency of the first (induction) from certainty is that it does not survey the particulars in a survey from which nothing escapes. But when the principles increase, probability strengthens, and the more the witnessing principles—i.e., the particulars—differ, the stronger the probability in it' (3). For example, the Shafi'is say: Wiping the head is an original function in ablution, so it is recommended to repeat it, and they relied in this on induction from washing the face, hands, and feet. When the Hanafis opposed them by saying 'It is a wiping that is not repeated' due to their induction from tayammum and wiping over the khuff, the Hanafis' probability was stronger due to the indication of two different particulars upon it, while the three limbs in ablution are in the ruling of a single witness due to their homogeneity. So in fiqh induction there is another element unknown to Aristotelian induction: preferring one series of particulars over another to reach the universal judgment. 'The deficiency of induction from perfection necessitated the deficiency of the resulting belief from certainty, and it did not necessitate the probability remaining in equilibrium, as it was, but rather it tipped the scales with probability for one of the two probabilities' (1). So fiqh induction has two elements: induction of series of particulars first, then preferring some of those series over others, and extracting the general judgment as a result of this preferring second. As for the weakness of analogical reasoning, it results from the major premise being probabilistic. Then al-Ghazali treats this reasoning with a purely usuli treatment (2). Then he discusses after that the syllogism of the cause (qiyas al-'illa) which is the syllogism of the cause (qiyas al-lim) among the logicians, and the syllogism of the indication (qiyas al-inn) among the logicians, and supports them with fiqh examples (3). So in al-Mi'yar there is criticism of the matter of premises, a criticism of human knowledge. And in his discussion of causality in this book he does not deny it in its absolute sense. Al-Mi'yar does not have the magnificent critical theory that al-Tahafut has; rather, it is an expression of a Greek spirit. As for Mihakk al-Nazar, the theological aspect does not dominate the book; rather, usuli researches prevail. He explains the syllogism of indication and cause (4). Then he speaks after that about the sources of fiqh syllogisms (5). It is noted about this book that al-Ghazali begins in...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly commentary on al-Ghazali's method of integrating logic into Islamic sciences, specifically fiqh. The author begins by quoting al-Ghazali's intention to use fiqh examples in his logic work 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' to make logical concepts accessible. Al-Ghazali anticipates criticism for using probabilistic (zanni) fiqh examples to explain certain rational matters, but defends this by analogizing to a carpenter who best understands examples from his own craft. The author explains that al-Ghazali sees this as the only way to make logic a tool (qanun) for sciences like fiqh, kalam, and philosophy. Al-Ghazali then analyzes incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) and analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili) as methods that yield only probability (zann), not certainty (yaqin), which is sufficient for fiqh. The author elaborates: the incompleteness of induction means it does not survey all particulars, but as supporting instances increase and differ, probability strengthens. An example from fiqh is given: the Shafi'i view that wiping the head in wudu is an original act recommended to be repeated, based on induction from washing the face, hands, and feet; the Hanafis counter with induction from tayammum and wiping the khuff that it is not repeated. The author notes that the Hanafis' probability is stronger because two different particulars support it, while the Shafi'i three are homogeneous. This introduces a key element unknown to Aristotelian induction: tarjih (preferencing) of one series of particulars over another to reach a universal judgment. Fiqh induction thus has two stages: collecting series of particulars, then preferring among them. The author then notes that analogical reasoning's weakness lies in its probabilistic major premise. Al-Ghazali treats this with usul principles, and also discusses qiyas al-'illa (syllogism of cause) and qiyas al-inn (syllogism of indication), supporting them with fiqh examples. The passage concludes by contrasting 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' (more Greek spirit) with 'Mihakk al-Nazar' (more usuli), and notes that in the latter, al-Ghazali explains qiyas al-dalala and qiyas al-'illa and discusses the sources of fiqh syllogisms.

Induction and Analogy
intermediate

Incomplete Induction and Tarjih in Fiqh Reasoning
Al-Ghazali analyzes incomplete induction and tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two-stage structure of fiqh induction (collecting particulars and tarjih) and apply it to a new fiqh dispute.

Core Idea
Fiqh induction adds a tarjih (preference) element absent in Aristotelian induction, making it a probabilistic reasoning tool.

Al-Ghazali analyzes incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) and analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili) as methods of fiqh that yield only probability (zann), not certainty (yaqin). He goes beyond Aristotelian induction by introducing a tarjih (preference) stage where different series of particulars are weighed. This lesson focuses on the fiqh induction example of wiping the head during wudu to show how tarjih works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two stages of fiqh induction
Al-Ghazali describes first collecting series of particulars, then preferring (tarjih) one series over another. This is the key innovation.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i-Hanafi example
The Shafi'is induce from washing face, hands, feet that wiping head is an original act (so repeated). Hanafis induce from tayammum and wiping khuff that wiping is not repeated.
3
Explain why Hanafis' probability is stronger
The passage says Hanafis have two different particulars (tayammum, khuff) while Shafi'is have three homogeneous ones (all limbs), so Hanafis' zann is stronger.
Homogeneous particulars count as a single witness.
4
Apply tarjih to a new case
Given a new fiqh dispute, identify the two series of particulars and determine which should be preferred based on diversity.
Diverse particulars provide stronger indication.

معيار العلم الإتجاه الأول ويقول الغزالي « رغبنا في أن نورد في منهاج الكلام في هذا الباب أمثلة فقهية فتشمل فائدته وتعم سائر الأصناف جدواه وعائدته(۱) ويشعر بأن هناك من سينكر عليه تفهم العقليات القطعية بالأمثلة الفقهية الظنية يقول « لعل الناظر بالعين العوراء نظر الطعن والإزراء - ينكر انحرافنا عن العادات في تفهيم العقليات القطعية بالأمثلة الفقهية الظنية ، فليكف عن غلوائه . في طعنه وإزرائه . وليشهد على نفسه بالجهل بصناعة التمثيل ، وفائدتها لم توضع إلا لتفهم الأمر الخفي بما هو الأعرف عند المخاطب المسترشد ، ليقيس مجهوله إلى ما هو معلوم عنده فيستقر المجهول في نفسه ، كان الخطاب مع نجار لا يحسن إلا النجر وكيفية استعمال آلاته ، وجب على مرشده ألا يضرب له المثل إلا من صناعة النجارة ، ليكون ذلك أسبق الى فهمه وأقرب إلى مناسبة عقله »(۲) . ويرى الغزالي أن هذه هي الطريقة الوحيدة لإدخال المنطق في تلك العلوم واتخاذه قانوناً لها . وكيف يتوصل الإنسان إلى المجهول إلا بما هو معلوم عنده ، فالفقیه بما لديه من فقه ، والمتكلم بما لديه من كلام ، والفيلسوف بما لديه من فلسفة . ولا يضع الغزالي أمثلة فقهية فقط في هذا الكتاب ، بل يحلل الإستقراء الناقص والقياس التمثيلي على أنهما طريقان من طرق الفقه التي لا توصل إلى اليقين بل إلى الظن ، وهذا هو المطلوب في الفقه . وقصور الأول عن اليقين أنه لم يتصفح الجزئيات تصفحاً لا يشذ عنه شيء ، ولكن إذا كثرت الأصول قوي الظن ، ومهما ازدادت الأصول الشاهدة - أعني الجزئيات - اختلافاً ، كان الظن أقوى فيه »(۳) فمثلاً يقول الشافعية : إن مسح الرأس وظيفة أصلية في الوضوء فيستحب تكرارها واستندوا في هذا إلى استقراء من غسل الوجه واليدين والرجلين . فاذا عارض الاحناف بأنه « مسح لا يكرر » لاستقرائهم ذلك من التيمم ومسح الخف ، كان ظن الاحناف أقوى لدلالة جزئين مختلفين عليه ، بينما الأعضاء الثلاثة في الوضوء في حكم شاهد واحد لتجانسهما . ففي الإستقراء الفقهي إذن عنصر آخر لم يعرفه الإستقراء الارسططاليسي ، هو ترجيح سلسلة من الجزئيات على سلسلة أخرى للوصول إلى الحكم الكلي « إن قصور الإستقراء عن الكمال أوجب قصور الاعتقاد الحاصل على اليقين ، ولم يوجب بقاء الاحتمال على التعادل ، كما كان ، بل رجح بالظن أحد الاحتمالين »(۱) . فالإستقراء الفقهي إذن عنصران : إستقراء سلاسل الجزئيات أولاً ، ثم ترجيح بعض تلك السلاسل على الأخرى ، واستخلاص الحكم العام نتيجة لهذا الترجيح ثانياً . أما ضعف القياس التمثيلي فناتج عن أن المقدمة الكبرى فيه ظنية . ثم يعالج الغزالي هذا القياس علاجاً أصولياً بحتاً(٢) . ثم يبحث بعد ذلك في قياس العلة وهو قياس اللم عند المنطقيين ، وقياس الإن عند المنطقيين ، ويدعمهما بأمثلة فقهية(۳) ، ففي المعيار إذن نقد لمادة المقدمات نقد للمعرفة الإنسانية . وهو في بحثه للعلية في هذا الكتاب لا ينكرها بمعناها المطلق . وليس في المعيار ـ ما في التهافت من نظرية نقدية رائعة ، بل هو تعبير عن روح يونانية . أما في محك النظر ، فلا يغلب على الكتاب الجانب الكلامي ، بل تسوده أبحاث أصولية . فيشرح قياس الدلالة والعلة(٤) . ثم يتكلم بعد ذلك عن مدارك الأقيسة الفقهية(٥) . ويلاحظ على هذا الكتاب أن الغزالي يبدأ في
The standard of knowledge: first direction. Al-Ghazali says: 'We desired to present in the curriculum of speech in this chapter fiqh examples so that its benefit may encompass and its utility and benefit may include all categories' (1). And he indicates that there are those who will criticize him for making certain rational matters understood through probabilistic fiqh examples. He says: 'Perhaps the beholder with the blind eye, the view of criticism and disparagement, will deny our deviation from customs in making certain rational matters understood through probabilistic fiqh examples. Let him cease his excess in his criticism and disparagement, and let him testify against himself as ignorant of the art of analogy. Its benefit is only placed to make a hidden matter understood through what is more known to the guided addressee, so that he may measure his unknown to what is known to him, and the unknown becomes settled in his soul. If the speech were to a carpenter who is only skilled in carpentry and the use of his tools, it would be incumbent on his guide not to strike an example for him except from the craft of carpentry, so that it may be quicker to his understanding and closer to the suitability of his mind' (2). Al-Ghazali sees that this is the only way to introduce logic into those sciences and adopt it as a law for them. How can a person attain the unknown except through what is known to him? So the jurist with his jurisprudence, the theologian with his theology, and the philosopher with his philosophy. Al-Ghazali does not place only fiqh examples in this book, but he analyzes incomplete induction and analogical reasoning as two methods of fiqh that do not lead to certainty but to probability, and this is what is required in fiqh. 'The deficiency of the first (induction) from certainty is that it does not survey the particulars in a survey from which nothing escapes. But when the principles increase, probability strengthens, and the more the witnessing principles—i.e., the particulars—differ, the stronger the probability in it' (3). For example, the Shafi'is say: Wiping the head is an original function in ablution, so it is recommended to repeat it, and they relied in this on induction from washing the face, hands, and feet. When the Hanafis opposed them by saying 'It is a wiping that is not repeated' due to their induction from tayammum and wiping over the khuff, the Hanafis' probability was stronger due to the indication of two different particulars upon it, while the three limbs in ablution are in the ruling of a single witness due to their homogeneity. So in fiqh induction there is another element unknown to Aristotelian induction: preferring one series of particulars over another to reach the universal judgment. 'The deficiency of induction from perfection necessitated the deficiency of the resulting belief from certainty, and it did not necessitate the probability remaining in equilibrium, as it was, but rather it tipped the scales with probability for one of the two probabilities' (1). So fiqh induction has two elements: induction of series of particulars first, then preferring some of those series over others, and extracting the general judgment as a result of this preferring second. As for the weakness of analogical reasoning, it results from the major premise being probabilistic. Then al-Ghazali treats this reasoning with a purely usuli treatment (2). Then he discusses after that the syllogism of the cause (qiyas al-'illa) which is the syllogism of the cause (qiyas al-lim) among the logicians, and the syllogism of the indication (qiyas al-inn) among the logicians, and supports them with fiqh examples (3). So in al-Mi'yar there is criticism of the matter of premises, a criticism of human knowledge. And in his discussion of causality in this book he does not deny it in its absolute sense. Al-Mi'yar does not have the magnificent critical theory that al-Tahafut has; rather, it is an expression of a Greek spirit. As for Mihakk al-Nazar, the theological aspect does not dominate the book; rather, usuli researches prevail. He explains the syllogism of indication and cause (4). Then he speaks after that about the sources of fiqh syllogisms (5). It is noted about this book that al-Ghazali begins in...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly commentary on al-Ghazali's method of integrating logic into Islamic sciences, specifically fiqh. The author begins by quoting al-Ghazali's intention to use fiqh examples in his logic work 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' to make logical concepts accessible. Al-Ghazali anticipates criticism for using probabilistic (zanni) fiqh examples to explain certain rational matters, but defends this by analogizing to a carpenter who best understands examples from his own craft. The author explains that al-Ghazali sees this as the only way to make logic a tool (qanun) for sciences like fiqh, kalam, and philosophy. Al-Ghazali then analyzes incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) and analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili) as methods that yield only probability (zann), not certainty (yaqin), which is sufficient for fiqh. The author elaborates: the incompleteness of induction means it does not survey all particulars, but as supporting instances increase and differ, probability strengthens. An example from fiqh is given: the Shafi'i view that wiping the head in wudu is an original act recommended to be repeated, based on induction from washing the face, hands, and feet; the Hanafis counter with induction from tayammum and wiping the khuff that it is not repeated. The author notes that the Hanafis' probability is stronger because two different particulars support it, while the Shafi'i three are homogeneous. This introduces a key element unknown to Aristotelian induction: tarjih (preferencing) of one series of particulars over another to reach a universal judgment. Fiqh induction thus has two stages: collecting series of particulars, then preferring among them. The author then notes that analogical reasoning's weakness lies in its probabilistic major premise. Al-Ghazali treats this with usul principles, and also discusses qiyas al-'illa (syllogism of cause) and qiyas al-inn (syllogism of indication), supporting them with fiqh examples. The passage concludes by contrasting 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' (more Greek spirit) with 'Mihakk al-Nazar' (more usuli), and notes that in the latter, al-Ghazali explains qiyas al-dalala and qiyas al-'illa and discusses the sources of fiqh syllogisms.

Key Terms
الاستقراء الناقص
القياس التمثيلي
الترجيح
الظن
اليقين
قياس العلة
قياس الدلالة

Checkpoint
In the example of wiping the head during wudu, why does al-Ghazali consider the Hanafis' probability (zann) stronger than that of the Shafi'is?
A) Because the Hanafis had more total particulars.
B) Because the Shafi'is' three particulars (face, hands, feet) are homogeneous and thus count as a single witness, whereas the Hanafis' two particulars (tayammum, khuff) are different and thus provide stronger indication.
C) Because the Hanafis used analogical reasoning instead of induction.
D) Because the Shafi'is' induction was complete.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh induction involves not only collecting particulars but also weighing different series (tarjih) to reach a stronger probabilistic ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Shafi'is cite hadiths of the Prophet raising his hands in many prayers; Hanafis cite hadiths where he did not. Induction and Analogy
beginner

The Two Components of Juridical Induction
Defines juridical induction with an example from wudu and tayammum, contrasting it with Aristotelian induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of juridical induction and distinguish it from Aristotelian induction.

Core Idea
Juridical induction adds preponderance of evidence to the simple collection of cases.

In legal reasoning, simply collecting examples is not enough when evidence conflicts. Juridical induction provides a method to weigh different series of cases and arrive at a probable ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'juridical induction'
Read the passage and locate where the author defines istiqra' fiqhi. Notice that it is contrasted with Aristotelian induction.
Juridical induction is specific to Islamic legal methodology.
2
Extract the two components
From the final sentence, find that juridical induction consists of: (1) induction of series of particulars, and (2) preponderance (tarjih) of some series over others to derive a general ruling.
The second component is unique to juridical induction.
3
Contrast with Aristotelian induction
The author states that preponderance is unknown to Aristotelian induction. Consider why: Aristotelean induction treats all particulars equally, while juridical induction weighs different series.
Aristotelian induction aims for universals by enumeration.
4
Apply to the wudu/tayammum example
The Hanafis have two diverse series (tayammum and wiping over khuff) both indicating that wiping is not repeated. The three limbs in wudu are homogeneous and count as one series. Preponderance goes to the Hanafi view because of diversity.
This illustrates how tarjih works in practice.

في هذا إلى استقراء من غسل الوجه واليدين والرجلين . فاذا عارض الاحناف بأنه « مسح لا يكرر » لاستقرائهم ذلك من التيمم ومسح الخف ، كان ظن الاحناف أقوى لدلالة جزئين مختلفين عليه ، بينما الأعضاء الثلاثة في الوضوء في حكم شاهد واحد لتجانسهما . ففي الإستقراء الفقهي إذن عنصر آخر لم يعرفه الإستقراء الارسططاليسي ، هو ترجيح سلسلة من الجزئيات على سلسلة أخرى للوصول إلى الحكم الكلي « إن قصور الإستقراء عن الكمال أوجب قصور الاعتقاد الحاصل على اليقين ، ولم يوجب بقاء الاحتمال على التعادل ، كما كان ، بل رجح بالظن أحد الاحتمالين »(۱) . فالإستقراء الفقهي إذن عنصران : إستقراء سلاسل الجزئيات أولاً ، ثم ترجيح بعض تلك السلاسل على الأخرى ، واستخلاص الحكم العام نتيجة لهذا الترجيح ثانياً .
In this to an induction from washing the face, hands, and feet. So when the Hanafis objected that it is 'a wipe that is not repeated' due to their induction from tayammum and wiping of the khuff, the opinion of the Hanafis was stronger because it indicates two different particulars, while the three limbs in wudu are in the status of a single witness due to their homogeneity. In juridical induction, therefore, there is another element unknown to Aristotelian induction, which is the preponderance of one series of particulars over another series to reach the universal judgment: 'The deficiency of induction from completeness necessitated deficiency of the belief obtained to certainty, and did not necessitate the remaining of probability on equilibrium, as it was, but rather it gave preponderance by opinion to one of the two probabilities.' So juridical induction consists of two elements: first, induction of series of particulars, then preponderance of some of those series over others, and extracting the general ruling as a result of this preponderance secondly.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines juridical induction (istiqra' fiqhi) and distinguishes it from Aristotelian induction. The author begins with an example from Islamic jurisprudence: The Hanafis argue that the wiping (masah) in wudu is not repeated because they induce from tayammum and wiping over khuff that wiping is not repeated. The majority (presumably Shafi'is) use the induction from the three limbs (face, hands, feet) which are washed, not wiped. The Hanafis' opinion is stronger because they have two different types of instances (tayammum and wiping over khuff) indicating the same ruling, whereas the three limbs in wudu are homogeneous and count as a single witness. This highlights that in juridical induction, the weight of different series of particulars is considered. The author then states that juridical induction contains an element unknown to Aristotelian induction: the preponderance (tarjih) of one series over another. A quote explains that the deficiency of induction (i.e., not covering all particulars) does not leave probability in equilibrium but tilts it toward one side by opinion (zann). Finally, the two elements of juridical induction are summarized: first, collecting series of particulars; second, weighing them and deriving the general ruling.

Key Terms
الإستقراء الفقهي (al-istiqra' al-fiqhi): Juridical induction: a method of deriving a general ruling by collecting multiple series of particular cases and giving preponderance to one series over another, based on the strength of evidence. [Note: This term is later developed in usul al-fiqh; here contrasted with Aristotelian induction.]
الترجيح (al-tarjih): The act of giving preponderance to one legal evidence over another when they conflict. [Note: Standard term in usul al-fiqh.]
الظن (al-zann): Probable opinion, not certain knowledge. [Note: Contrasted with yaqin (certainty).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In juridical induction, what distinguishes it from Aristotelian induction?
A) It relies on a single witness
B) It weighs multiple series of particulars against each other
C) It only uses analogical reasoning
D) It always produces certainty

Key Takeaway
Juridical induction consists of collecting series of particulars and then giving preponderance to one series over another to reach a probable ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the method of juridical induction, evaluate the strength of the Hanafi and Shafi'i evidence in the wudu wiping dispute. Which series is stronger and why?

Induction and Analogy

Weighing Evidence: Tarjih in Juridical Induction
Defines juridical induction with an example from wudu and tayammum, contrasting it with Aristotelian induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how tarjih (preponderance) tilts probability in juridical induction and why the Hanafis' evidence was considered stronger.

Core Idea
Preponderance transforms balanced probability into a tilted opinion (zann).

When two conflicting inductions arise, the jurist does not remain in doubt; instead, the evidence that has more support across different series is given preference, leading to a ruling based on probable opinion. This lesson explores the concept of tarjih through the wudu example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting series in the example
The Hanafis base their ruling on two series: tayammum and wiping over khuff. The Shafi'is base theirs on the three limbs washed in wudu. Both series aim to determine whether wiping in wudu is repeated.
Series are collections of particulars that point to a general ruling.
2
Note the diversity of cases in each series
The Hanafi series includes two different types of acts (tayammum and khuff) that both involve wiping. The Shafi'i series includes three limbs, but they are all similar (washed, not wiped).
Diversity strengthens the inductive evidence because it shows the ruling applies across different contexts.
3
Understand preponderance (tarjih)
The author states that the Hanafis' opinion is stronger because it indicates two different particulars (two different series), while the three limbs count as one witness. This weighing of evidence is tarjih.
Tarjih is the key second component of juridical induction.
4
Recognize the epistemological outcome
The passage explains that the deficiency of induction (incompleteness) does not leave probability in equilibrium; rather, tarjih tilts it toward one side, producing a probable opinion (zann), not certainty.
Zann is sufficient for legal rulings in many cases.

Induction and Analogy
intermediate

Weakness of Analogical Reasoning
Critiques the probabilistic nature of analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili) yields only probabilistic conclusions and critique its major premise.

Core Idea
Analogical reasoning's major premise is probabilistic, making its conclusions uncertain.

Logic differentiates between certain and probable reasoning. Analogical reasoning moves from one particular to another based on a shared attribute, but its major premise is only probable. This lesson explores why this is a weakness and how it differs from causal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of analogical reasoning
Identify the three components: two particulars and a shared attribute. The major premise links the attribute to a ruling or property.
2
Identify the major premise
In analogical reasoning, the major premise is a general statement that the shared attribute is connected to the conclusion. For example, 'Everything intoxicating is forbidden' is the major premise.
3
See why the major premise is probabilistic
The major premise is based on observed instances, not a universal causal connection. It is zanni (probable) because it relies on induction or similarity rather than necessity.
4
Recognize the consequence
Because the major premise is only probable, the conclusion inherits probability; analogical reasoning cannot yield yaqin (certainty).

أما ضعف القياس التمثيلي فناتج عن أن المقدمة الكبرى فيه ظنية . ثم يعالج الغزالي هذا القياس علاجاً أصولياً بحتاً(٢) . ثم يبحث بعد ذلك في قياس العلة وهو قياس اللم عند المنطقيين ، وقياس الإن عند المنطقيين ، ويدعمهما بأمثلة فقهية(۳) ، ففي المعيار إذن نقد لمادة المقدمات نقد للمعرفة الإنسانية . وهو في بحثه للعلية في هذا الكتاب لا ينكرها بمعناها المطلق . وليس في المعيار ـ ما في التهافت من نظرية نقدية رائعة ، بل هو تعبير عن روح يونانية .

Ghazali's Treatment and Types of Qiyas
Describes Ghazali's usul-based treatment and introduces causal and sign syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qiyas al-'illa (causal syllogism) and qiyas al-inna (syllogism of sign) and explain how Ghazali's usul-based approach strengthens analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Ghazali treats analogical reasoning by transforming it into a causal syllogism rooted in usul al-fiqh.

After critiquing analogical reasoning, al-Ghazali does not discard it but treats it with a purely usul-based method. He then examines two types of syllogisms: qiyas al-'illa (causal) and qiyas al-inna (sign), supporting them with fiqh examples. This lesson dives into these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ghazali's usul-based treatment
Ghazali approaches analogical reasoning using principles from usul al-fiqh, grounding the analogy in a recognized 'illa (effective cause) rather than mere similarity.
2
Define qiyas al-'illa (qiyas al-lamm)
Here the middle term is the cause of the predicate. It is a demonstration proper: the cause explains why the predicate holds.
3
Define qiyas al-inna
Here the middle term is a sign or effect of the predicate. It shows existence but not the reason (demonstration of existence).
4
Compare both types using fiqh examples
In fiqh, qiyas al-'illa uses the cause (e.g., intoxication) to extend a ruling; qiyas al-inna uses a sign (e.g., the smell of intoxicant) as evidence.

Source Text

Epistemological Critique in Mi'yar and Contrast with Tahafut
Analyzes the epistemological critique in Mi'yar and contrasts with Tahafut.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the epistemological critique of premises in Mi'yar and explain how it differs from the radical critique in Tahafut al-Falasifa.

Core Idea
Mi'yar critiques the material of premises without denying causality, embodying a Greek spirit rather than the radical critique of Tahafut.

The final part of the passage highlights that Mi'yar contains a critique of the matter of premises, a critique of human knowledge, but does not go as far as Tahafut's brilliant critical theory. Ghazali in Mi'yar does not deny causality absolutely and expresses a Greek spirit. This lesson explores the nature of Mi'yar's critique.

Guided Path
1
Recognize that Mi'yar critiques the material of premises
The passage says there is a critique of the matter of premises, meaning an examination of the quality and certainty of the statements used in arguments.
2
Note that Ghazali does not deny causality absolutely
Unlike some philosophers, Ghazali in Mi'yar does not reject causality outright; he maintains a framework where cause and effect are accepted.
3
Contrast with Tahafut's radical critique
Tahafut al-Falasifa contains a brilliant critical theory that challenges the very foundations of Greek philosophy, including causality, whereas Mi'yar stays within the Greek logical tradition.
4
Evaluate what 'Greek spirit' means
The phrase indicates that Mi'yar adopts the Aristotelian logical framework, focusing on formalism and demonstration, rather than a revolutionary epistemic critique.

Third Argument: Induction Shows People Know Without Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya uses induction to argue that people acquire concepts without definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's inductive argument against the necessity of definitions and evaluate its strength using the criteria of inductive logic.

Core Idea
Induction proves that most concepts are acquired without definitions.

Ibn Taymiyya's third argument shifts from theoretical reasoning to empirical observation. He uses induction (istiqra') to examine how people actually acquire concepts across cultures and disciplines. If we find that they do so without formal definitions, then the logicians' claim that definitions are necessary collapses. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Determine the claim Ibn Taymiyya aims to prove: that conceptualization (tasawwur) is independent of definition (hadd).
The conclusion is stated explicitly: 'In this is evidence of the independence of conceptualization from definition.'
2
Identify the premises
Locate the evidence he uses: induction of people's conditions in various nations, sciences, and crafts, showing they do not resort to definitions.
The premise is that people across different fields form concepts without definitions.
3
Determine the logical form
Recognize this as an inductive argument: generalizing from observed particular cases to a universal claim about all people and all concepts.
Induction moves from many instances to a general rule, but does not guarantee certainty.
4
Assess inductive strength
Evaluate the breadth of the sample: Ibn Taymiyya mentions 'various nations and various sciences and crafts' but does not claim completeness. Is this sufficient to support the conclusion?
Logicians would consider an incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) insufficient for certainty; Ibn Taymiyya uses it as a persuasive tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن استقراء أحوال الناس في مختلف الأمم وفي مختلف العلوم والصناعات يثبت أنهم مع تصورهم لمفردات علومهم وصناعاتهم ، لم يلجأوا إلى الحدود في التوصل إليها . وفي هذا دليل على استغناء التصور عن الحد (٢) .
The third argument: Indeed, induction of the conditions of people in various nations and in various sciences and crafts proves that, with their conceptualization of the terms of their sciences and crafts, they did not resort to definitions in arriving at them. And in this is evidence of the independence of conceptualization from definition.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's third argument, which is based on induction (istiqra'). He claims that by observing people across different nations, sciences, and crafts, we find that they acquire concepts (tasawwur) of the terms in their fields without using formal Aristotelian definitions. This empirical evidence, he argues, shows that conceptualization is independent of definition. The implication is that definitions are not necessary for knowledge, contra the logicians' claim. The argument is inductive in nature: generalizing from observed cases to a universal conclusion.

Key Terms
استقراء
تصور

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyya's Inductive Argument Against the Necessity of Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya uses induction to argue that concepts are attained without definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's inductive argument and identify how it undermines the claim that definitions are necessary for concept formation.

Core Idea
Induction shows that people gain concepts without definitions, refuting the logicians' claim.

Ibn Taymiyya presents a powerful empirical argument against the logicians' doctrine that definitions are required for attaining clear concepts. By observing real-world practice across nations and disciplines, he argues that concepts exist independently of formal definitions. Let's walk through his induction and see why it challenges a core assumption of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The logicians assert that definitions (hudud) are necessary for attaining concepts (tasawwurat). Ibn Taymiyya targets this claim.
2
Understand the inductive method
Ibn Taymiyya uses istiqra' (induction) by examining the conditions of people across various nations, sciences, and industries.
Istiqra' here is empirical observation, not a formal inductive syllogism.
3
Observe the instances
He notes that people in these diverse contexts possess concepts of the terms in their fields without ever having used definitions. For example, a carpenter understands 'wood' and a jurist understands 'sale' without formal definitions.
These are concrete examples of concept attainment without definitions.
4
Generalize the conclusion
From these observed instances, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that concepts are generally independent of definitions. This serves as evidence (dalil) against the necessity of definitions.
The induction is not complete (tamm) but is presented as a strong empirical generalization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن استقراء أحوال الناس في مختلف الأمم وفي مختلف العلوم والصناعات يثبت أنهم مع تصورهم لمفردات علومهم وصناعاتهم ، لم يلجأوا إلى الحدود في التوصل إليها . وفي هذا دليل على استغناء التصور عن الحد.
The third argument: The induction of the conditions of people in various nations and in various sciences and industries proves that while they have concepts of the terms of their sciences and industries, they did not resort to definitions in attaining them. And in this is evidence for the self-sufficiency of concept from definition.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument, which is inductive (istiqra'). Ibn Taymiyya argues that by observing people across different nations, sciences, and crafts, we see that they possess concepts (tasawwurat) of the terms used in their fields without ever having relied on formal definitions. For example, a carpenter knows what 'wood' is without a definition, or a jurist knows 'sale' without a hadd. This empirical observation demonstrates that concepts can be attained independently of definitions, thus refuting the claim that definition is necessary for concept formation.
